style="text-align:left;padding-right:0.2em"> <a href="/wiki/Ghurid_dynasty" title="Ghurid dynasty">Ghurid dynasty</a></td><td style="text-align:right;font-size:90%;white-space:nowrap"> pre-879 – 1215</td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="text-align:left;padding-right:0.2em"> <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_Empire" title="Seljuk Empire">Seljuk Empire</a></td><td style="text-align:right;font-size:90%;white-space:nowrap"> 1037–1194</td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="text-align:left;padding-right:0.2em"> <a href="/wiki/Khwarazmian_dynasty" title="Khwarazmian dynasty">Khwarazmian dynasty</a></td><td style="text-align:right;font-size:90%;white-space:nowrap"> 1077–1231</td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="text-align:left;padding-right:0.2em"> <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Rum" title="Sultanate of Rum">Sultanate of Rum</a></td><td style="text-align:right;font-size:90%;white-space:nowrap"> 1077–1307</td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="text-align:left;padding-right:0.2em"> <a href="/wiki/Salghurids" title="Salghurids">Salghurids</a></td><td style="text-align:right;font-size:90%;white-space:nowrap"> 1148–1282</td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="text-align:left;padding-right:0.2em"> <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a></td><td style="text-align:right;font-size:90%;white-space:nowrap"> 1256–1353</td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="text-align:left;padding-right:0.2em"> <a style="vertical-align:top"><td style="text-align:left;padding-right:0.2em"> <a href="/wiki/Jalairid_Sultanate" title="Jalairid Sultanate">Jalairid Sultanate</a></td><td 1407–1468</td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="text-align:left;padding-right:0.2em"> <a href="/wiki/Aq_Qoyunlu" title="Aq Qoyunlu">Aq Qoyunlu Turcomans</a></td><td style="text-align:right;font-size:90%;white-space:nowrap"> 1378–1508</td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="text-align:left;padding-right:0.2em"> <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" 1526–1857</td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="text-align:left;padding-right:0.2em"> <a href="/wiki/Hotak_dynasty" title="Hotak dynasty">Hotak dynasty</a></td><td 1750–1794</td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="text-align:left;padding-right:0.2em"> <a href="/wiki/Durrani_Empire" title="Durrani Empire">Durrani Empire</a></td><td dynasty">Qajar dynasty</a></td><td style="text-align:right;font-size:90%;white-space:nowrap"> 1794–1925</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;padding-top:0;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_Greater_Iran" title="Template:History of Greater Iran"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_Greater_Iran" title="Template talk:History of Greater Iran"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>

<table role="presentation" class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Farsi.svg/40px-Farsi.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="29" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Farsi.svg/60px-Farsi.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Farsi.svg/80px-Farsi.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="323" data-file-height="235" /></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/Persian_alphabet" title="Persian alphabet">Persian text</a>.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Arabic" title="Help:Arabic">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a>.</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p><b>Greater Iran</b> (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span lang="fa" dir="rtl">ایران بزرگ</span>&#8206;, <i lang="fa-Latn" title="Persian-language romanization">Irān-e Bozorg</i>) is a term used to refer to the <a href="/wiki/Region" title="Region">regions</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Asia" class="mw-redirect" title="West Asia">West Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, and parts of <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> that have significant <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Iran" title="Culture of Iran">Iranian</a> cultural influence due to having been either long historically ruled by the various imperial dynasties of the Iranian Empire (such as those of the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samanid_Empire" title="Samanid Empire">Samanids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Afsharid_dynasty" title="Afsharid dynasty">Afsharids</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Qajar_dynasty" title="Qajar dynasty">Qajars</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-Marcinkowski_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marcinkowski-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> having considerable aspects of <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Iran" title="Culture of Iran">Persian culture</a> due to extensive contact with the various imperial dynasties of Iran (e.g., those regions and peoples in the <a href="/wiki/North_Caucasus" title="North Caucasus">North Caucasus</a> that were not under direct Iranian rule), or are simply nowadays still inhabited by a significant amount of <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian peoples</a> who patronize their respective cultures (as it goes for the western parts of <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>). It roughly corresponds to the territory on the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_plateau" class="mw-redirect" title="Iranian plateau">Iranian plateau</a> and its bordering <a href="/wiki/Plain" title="Plain">plains</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IRAN_i._LANDS_OF_IRAN_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IRAN_i._LANDS_OF_IRAN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Iranica" title="Encyclopædia Iranica">Encyclopædia Iranica</a> uses the term <i>Iranian Cultural Continent</i>  for this region.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term Greater Iran is not limited to the modern state of Iran, but includes all the territory ruled by the Iranians throughout the history, including <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, Eastern Anatolia, all of the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The concept of Greater Iran has its source in the history of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> in <a href="/wiki/Persis" title="Persis">Persis</a> (modern day <a href="/wiki/Fars_province" class="mw-redirect" title="Fars province">Pars region</a>), and overlaps to a certain extent with the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iran" title="History of Iran">history of Iran</a>.
</p><p>In recent centuries, Iran lost many of the territories conquered under the <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qajar_dynasty" title="Qajar dynasty">Qajar</a> dynasties, including Iraq to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a> (via the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Amasya" title="Peace of Amasya">Treaty of Amasya</a> in 1555 and the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Zuhab" title="Treaty of Zuhab">Treaty of Zuhab</a> in 1639), western Afghanistan to the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> (via the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1857)" title="Treaty of Paris (1857)">Treaty of Paris</a> in 1857<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and the MacMahon Arbitration in 1905<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>), and all its <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> territories to <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Persian_Wars" title="Russo-Persian Wars">Russo-Persian Wars</a> in the course of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Gulistan" title="Treaty of Gulistan">Treaty of Gulistan</a> in 1813 resulted in <a href="/wiki/Qajar_Iran" title="Qajar Iran">Iran</a> ceding <a href="/wiki/Dagestan" title="Dagestan">Dagestan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, and most of <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> to <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Turkmenchay" title="Treaty of Turkmenchay">Turkmanchey Treaty</a> of 1828 decisively ended centuries of Iranian control of its Caucasian provinces,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFisherAveryHamblyMelville1991329_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFisherAveryHamblyMelville1991329-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and made Iran cede what is present-day <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, the remainder of <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Igdir_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Igdir Province">Igdir</a> (eastern Turkey), and set the modern boundary along the <a href="/wiki/Aras_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Aras River">Aras River</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On the <a href="/wiki/Nowruz" title="Nowruz">Nowruz</a> of 1935, the <a href="/wiki/Exonym_and_endonym" title="Exonym and endonym">endonym</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Name_of_Iran" title="Name of Iran">Iran</a></i> was adopted as the official international name of <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persia</a> by its ruler <a href="/wiki/Reza_Shah_Pahlavi" class="mw-redirect" title="Reza Shah Pahlavi">Reza Shah Pahlavi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> However, in 1959, the government of <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Reza_Shah_Pahlavi" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohammad Reza Shah Pahlavi">Mohammad Reza Shah Pahlavi</a>, Reza Shah Pahlavi's son, announced that both "Persia" and "Iran" could officially be used interchangeably.<sup id="cite_ref-yarshater1_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yarshater1-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The name "Irān", meaning "land of the <a href="/wiki/Aryan" title="Aryan">Aryans</a>", is the <a href="/wiki/New_Persian" class="mw-redirect" title="New Persian">New Persian</a> continuation of the old genitive plural <i>aryānām</i> (proto-Iranian, meaning "of the Aryans"), first attested in the <a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">Avesta</a> as <i>airyānąm</i> (the text of which is composed in <a href="/wiki/Avestan_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Avestan language">Avestan</a>, an old <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian language</a> spoken in northeastern Greater Iran, or in what are now <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopaedia_Iranica1_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopaedia_Iranica1-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopaedia_Iranica_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopaedia_Iranica-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The proto-Iranian term <i>aryānām</i> is present in the term <i><a href="/wiki/Airyanem_Vaejah" title="Airyanem Vaejah">Airyana Vaēǰah</a></i>, the homeland of <a href="/wiki/Zoroaster" title="Zoroaster">Zoroaster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>, near the provinces of <a href="/wiki/Sogdiana" class="mw-redirect" title="Sogdiana">Sogdiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Margiana" title="Margiana">Margiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>, etc., listed in the first chapter of the <a href="/wiki/Vendidad" title="Vendidad">Vidēvdād</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The Avestan evidence is confirmed by <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> sources: <a href="/wiki/Ariana" title="Ariana">Arianē</a> is spoken of as being between <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopaedia_Iranica2_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopaedia_Iranica2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> However, this is a Greek pronunciation of the name Haroyum/Haraiva (<a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a>), which the Greeks called 'Aria'.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> A land listed separately from the homeland of the Aryans.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While up until the end of the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian period</a> in the 3rd century CE, the idea of "Irān" had an ethnic, linguistic, and religious value, it did not yet have a political import. The idea of an "Iranian" empire or kingdom in a political sense is a purely <a href="/wiki/Sasanian" class="mw-redirect" title="Sasanian">Sasanian</a> one. It was the result of a convergence of interests between the new dynasty and the <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Zoroastrian">Zoroastrian</a> clergy, as we can deduce from the available evidence. This convergence gave rise to the idea of an Ērān-šahr "Kingdom of the Iranians", which was "ēr" (<a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">Middle Persian</a> equivalent of <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a> "ariya" and Avestan "airya").<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopaedia_Iranica2_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopaedia_Iranica2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Nelson_Frye" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard Nelson Frye">Richard Nelson Frye</a> defines Greater Iran as including "much of the Caucasus, Iraq, Afghanistan, Pakistan and Central Asia, with cultural influences extending to China and western India." According to Frye, "Iran means all lands and peoples where Iranian languages were and are spoken, and where in the past, multi-faceted Iranian cultures existed."<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Foltz" title="Richard Foltz">Richard Foltz</a> notes that while "A general assumption is often made that the various Iranian peoples of 'greater Iran'—a cultural area that stretched from Mesopotamia and the Caucasus into <a href="/wiki/Khwarizm" class="mw-redirect" title="Khwarizm">Khwarizm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transoxiana" title="Transoxiana">Transoxiana</a>, Bactria, and the <a href="/wiki/Pamirs" class="mw-redirect" title="Pamirs">Pamirs</a> and included Persians, Medes, Parthians and Sogdians among others—were all 'Zoroastrians' in pre-Islamic times... This view, even though common among serious scholars, is almost certainly overstated." Foltz argues that "While the various Iranian peoples did indeed share a common <a href="/wiki/Pantheon_(gods)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pantheon (gods)">pantheon</a> and pool of religious myths and <a href="/wiki/Religious_symbol" title="Religious symbol">symbols</a>, in actuality a variety of deities were worshipped—particularly <a href="/wiki/Mitra" title="Mitra">Mitra</a>, the god of covenants, and <a href="/wiki/Anahita" title="Anahita">Anahita</a>, the goddess of the waters, but also many others—depending on the time, place, and particular group concerned".<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
To the Ancient Greeks, Greater Iran ended at the Indus River located in <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/J._P._Mallory" title="J. P. Mallory">J. P. Mallory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Q._Adams" title="Douglas Q. Adams">Douglas Q. Adams</a> most of Western <i>greater Iran</i> spoke Southwestern Iranian languages in the Achaemenid era while the Eastern territory spoke Eastern Iranian languages related to Avestan.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>George Lane also states that after the dissolution of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanids" class="mw-redirect" title="Ilkhanids">Ilkhanids</a> became rulers of greater Iran<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Uljaytu" class="mw-redirect" title="Uljaytu">Uljaytu</a>, according to Judith G. Kolbas, was the ruler of this expanse between 1304–1317 A.D.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Primary sources, including Timurid historian Mir Khwand, define Iranshahr (Greater Iran) as extending from the Euphrates to the Oxus<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditionally, and until recent times, ethnicity has never been a defining separating criterion  in these regions. In the words of Richard Nelson Frye:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Many times I have emphasized that the present peoples of Central Asia, whether Iranian or Turkic speaking, have one culture, one religion, one set of social values and traditions with only language separating them.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Richard Nelson Frye</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Only in modern times did western colonial intervention and ethnicity tend to become a dividing force between the provinces of Greater Iran. As <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Clawson" title="Patrick Clawson">Patrick Clawson</a> states, "ethnic nationalism is largely a nineteenth century phenomenon, even if it is fashionable to retroactively extend it."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> "Greater Iran" however has been more of a cultural super-state, rather than a political one to begin with.
</p><p>In the work <i>Nuzhat al-Qolub</i> (<span lang="fa" title="Persian language text"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" title="Persian Nastaliq" style="font-family: &#39;IranNastaliq&#39;, &#39;Urdu Emad Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta3&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta2&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta1&#39;, &#39;Urdu Typesetting&#39;, &#39;Jameel Noori Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Hussaini Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01&#39;, &#39;Pak Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;PDMS_Jauhar&#39;, &#39;Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq&#39;; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;">نزهه القلوب</span></span>), the medieval geographer <a href="/wiki/Hamdallah_Mustawfi" title="Hamdallah Mustawfi">Hamdallah Mustawfi</a> wrote:
</p><p><span lang="fa" title="Persian language text"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" title="Persian Nastaliq" style="font-family: &#39;IranNastaliq&#39;, &#39;Urdu Emad Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta3&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta2&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta1&#39;, &#39;Urdu Typesetting&#39;, &#39;Jameel Noori Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Hussaini Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01&#39;, &#39;Pak Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;PDMS_Jauhar&#39;, &#39;Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq&#39;; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;">چند شهر است اندر ایران مرتفع تر از همه</span></span><br />
<i>Some cities in Iran are above the rest,</i><br />
<span lang="fa" title="Persian language text"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" title="Persian Nastaliq" style="font-family: &#39;IranNastaliq&#39;, &#39;Urdu Emad Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta3&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta2&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta1&#39;, &#39;Urdu Typesetting&#39;, &#39;Jameel Noori Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Hussaini Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01&#39;, &#39;Pak Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;PDMS_Jauhar&#39;, &#39;Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq&#39;; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;">بهتر و سازنده تر از خوشی آب و هوا</span></span><br />
<i>better and more productive due to good weather,</i><br />
<span lang="fa" title="Persian language text"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" title="Persian Nastaliq" style="font-family: &#39;IranNastaliq&#39;, &#39;Urdu Emad Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta3&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta2&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta1&#39;, &#39;Urdu Typesetting&#39;, &#39;Jameel Noori Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Hussaini Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01&#39;, &#39;Pak Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;PDMS_Jauhar&#39;, &#39;Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq&#39;; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;">گنجه پر گنج در اران صفاهان در عراق</span></span><br />
<i>Ganja full of treasure in <a href="/wiki/Arran_(Caucasus)" title="Arran (Caucasus)">Arran</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Esfah%C4%81n" class="mw-redirect" title="Esfahān">Esfahān</a> in <a href="/wiki/Persian_Iraq" title="Persian Iraq">Iraq</a>,</i><br />
<span lang="fa" title="Persian language text"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" title="Persian Nastaliq" style="font-family: &#39;IranNastaliq&#39;, &#39;Urdu Emad Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta3&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta2&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta1&#39;, &#39;Urdu Typesetting&#39;, &#39;Jameel Noori Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Hussaini Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01&#39;, &#39;Pak Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;PDMS_Jauhar&#39;, &#39;Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq&#39;; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;">در خراسان مرو و طوس در روم باشد اقسرا</span></span><br />
<i><a href="/wiki/Merv" title="Merv">Merv</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tus,_Iran" title="Tus, Iran">Tus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khorasan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Konya" title="Konya">Konya</a> (Aqsara) in Rome (Anatolia).</i>
</p><p>The <i>Cambridge History of Iran</i> takes a geographical approach in referring to the "historical and cultural" entity of "Greater Iran" as "areas of Iran, parts of Afghanistan, and Chinese and Soviet Central Asia".<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> A detailed list of these territories follows in this article.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Persian_literature">In Persian literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: In Persian literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-Empty_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section is empty.</b> <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2016</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Greater Iran is called <i>Iranzamin</i> (<span lang="fa" title="Persian language text"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" title="Persian Nastaliq" style="font-family: &#39;IranNastaliq&#39;, &#39;Urdu Emad Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta3&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta2&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta1&#39;, &#39;Urdu Typesetting&#39;, &#39;Jameel Noori Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Hussaini Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01&#39;, &#39;Pak Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;PDMS_Jauhar&#39;, &#39;Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq&#39;; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;">ایرانزمین</span></span>) which means "The Land of Iran". <i>Iranzamin</i> was in the mythical times opposed to the <i>Turanzamin</i> the Land of <a href="/wiki/Turan" title="Turan">Turan</a>, which was located in the upper part of Central Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the pre-Islamic period, Iranians distinguished two main regions in the territory they ruled, one Iran and the other <i>Aniran</i>. By Iran they meant all the regions inhabited by <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Iranian_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Iranian peoples">ancient Iranian peoples</a>, this region was more extensive in the past. This notion of <i>Iran</i> as a territory (opposed to <i>Aniran</i>) can be seen as the core of early Greater Iran. Later many changes occurred in the boundaries and areas where Iranians lived but the languages and culture remained the dominant medium in many parts of the Greater Iran.
</p><p>As an example, the Persian language (referred to, in Persian, as <i>Farsi</i>) was the main literary language and the language of correspondence in Central Asia and Caucasus prior to the Russian occupation, Central Asia being the birthplace of modern Persian language. Furthermore, according to the British government, Persian language was also used in <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Kurdistan" title="Iraqi Kurdistan">Iraqi Kurdistan</a>, prior to the British Occupation and Mandate in 1918-1932.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Russia">Imperial Russia</a> continuously advancing south in the course of two wars against Persia, and the treaties of Turkmenchay and Gulistan in the western frontiers, plus the unexpected death of <a href="/wiki/Abbas_Mirza" title="Abbas Mirza">Abbas Mirza</a> in 1823, and the murdering of Persia's Grand <a href="/wiki/Vizier" title="Vizier">Vizier</a> (Mirza AbolQasem Qa'im Maqām), many Central Asian khanates began losing hope for any support from Persia against the <a href="/wiki/Tsar" title="Tsar">Tsarist</a> armies.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The Russian armies occupied the <a href="/wiki/Aral_Sea" title="Aral Sea">Aral</a> coast in 1849, <a href="/wiki/Tashkent" title="Tashkent">Tashkent</a> in 1864, <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a> in 1867, <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a> in 1868, and <a href="/wiki/Khiva" title="Khiva">Khiva</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amudarya" class="mw-redirect" title="Amudarya">Amudarya</a> in 1873.
</p>
<dl><dd><i>"Many Iranians consider their natural sphere of influence to extend beyond Iran's present borders. After all, Iran was once much larger. Portuguese forces seized islands and ports in the 16th and 17th centuries. In the 19th century, the Russian Empire wrested from <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">Tehran</a>'s control what is today Armenia, <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Azerbaijan" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of Azerbaijan">Republic of Azerbaijan</a>, and part of Georgia. Iranian elementary school texts teach about the Iranian roots not only of cities like <a href="/wiki/Baku" title="Baku">Baku</a>, but also cities further north like <a href="/wiki/Derbent" title="Derbent">Derbent</a> in southern Russia. The <a href="/wiki/Shah" title="Shah">Shah</a> lost much of his claim to western Afghanistan following the Anglo-Iranian war of 1856-1857. Only in 1970 did a <a href="/wiki/UN" class="mw-redirect" title="UN">UN</a> sponsored consultation end Iranian claims to <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerainty</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> island nation of <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>. In centuries past, Iranian rule once stretched westward into modern Iraq and beyond. When the western world complains of Iranian interference beyond its borders, the Iranian government often convinced itself that it is merely exerting its influence in lands that were once its own. Simultaneously, Iran's losses at the hands of outside powers have contributed to a sense of grievance that continues to the present day."</i> -<a href="/wiki/Patrick_Clawson" title="Patrick Clawson">Patrick Clawson</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Institute_for_Near_East_Policy" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Institute for Near East Policy">Washington Institute for Near East Policy</a><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><i>"Iran today is just a rump of what it once was. At its height, Iranian rulers controlled Iraq, Afghanistan, Western Pakistan, much of Central Asia, and the Caucasus. Many Iranians today consider these areas part of a greater Iranian sphere of influence."</i> -<a href="/wiki/Patrick_Clawson" title="Patrick Clawson">Patrick Clawson</a><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><i>"Since the days of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenids" class="mw-redirect" title="Achaemenids">Achaemenids</a>, the Iranians had the protection of geography. But high mountains and vast emptiness of the Iranian plateau were no longer enough to shield Iran from the Russian army or British navy. Both literally, and figuratively, Iran shrank. At the beginning of the nineteenth century, Azerbaijan, Armenia, and Afghanistan were Iranian, but by the end of the century, all this territory had been lost as a result of European military action."</i><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Provinces_and_regions">Provinces and regions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Provinces and regions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the 8th century, Iran was conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Abbassids" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbassids">Abbassids</a> who ruled from <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, and the territory of Iran at that time was known to be composed of two portions: <i><a href="/wiki/Persian_Iraq" title="Persian Iraq">Persian Iraq</a></i> (western portion) and <i>Khorasan</i> (eastern portion). The dividing region was mostly along with <a href="/wiki/Gurgan" class="mw-redirect" title="Gurgan">Gurgan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Damghan" title="Damghan">Damaghan</a> cities. Especially the <a href="/wiki/Ghaznavids" title="Ghaznavids">Ghaznavids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seljuq_dynasty" title="Seljuq dynasty">Seljuqs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timurid_dynasty" title="Timurid dynasty">Timurids</a> divided their <a href="/wiki/Empire" title="Empire">Empire</a> to Iraqi and Khorasani regions. This point can be observed in many books such as <i>"Tārīkhi Baïhaqī"</i> of <a href="/wiki/Abolfazl_Beyhaqi" class="mw-redirect" title="Abolfazl Beyhaqi">Abul Fazl Bayhqi</a>, <i>Faza'ilul al-anam min rasa'ili hujjat al-Islam</i> (a collection of letters of <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al-Ghazali</a>) and other books. Transoxiana and <a href="/wiki/Chorasmia" class="mw-redirect" title="Chorasmia">Chorasmia</a> were mostly included in the Khorasanian region.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_East">Middle East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Middle East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bahrain">Bahrain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Bahrain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Persians_in_Bahrain" class="mw-redirect" title="Persians in Bahrain">Persians in Bahrain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huwala" class="mw-redirect" title="Huwala">Huwala</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ajam_of_Bahrain" title="Ajam of Bahrain">Ajam of Bahrain</a></div>
<p>The "Ajam" and "Huwala" are ethnic communities of Bahrain of Persian origin. The Persians of Bahrain are a significant and influential ethnic community whose ancestors arrived in Bahrain within the last 1,000 years as laborers, merchants and artisans. They have traditionally been merchants living in specific quarters of <a href="/wiki/Manama" title="Manama">Manama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muharraq" title="Muharraq">Muharraq</a>. Bahrain's Persians who adhere to the <a href="/wiki/Shia" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia">Shia</a> sect of Islam are <a href="/wiki/Ajam" title="Ajam">Ajam</a> and the Persians who adhere to the <a href="/wiki/Sunni" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> sect are called <a href="/wiki/Huwala" class="mw-redirect" title="Huwala">Huwala</a>, who migrated from <a href="/wiki/Larestan" class="mw-redirect" title="Larestan">Larestan</a> in Iran to the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> in the seventeenth and eighteenth century.
</p><p>The immigration of Persians to Bahrain began when the Greek <a href="/wiki/Seleucid" class="mw-redirect" title="Seleucid">Seleucid</a> kingdom which was ruling Bahrain at the time fell and the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persian Empire</a> successfully invaded Bahrain, but it is often believed that mass immigration started during the 1600s when <a href="/wiki/Abbas_I_of_Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbas I of Persia">Abbas I of Persia</a> invaded Bahrain. After settling in Bahrain, some of the Persians were effectively Arabized. They usually settled in areas inhabited by the indigenous <a href="/wiki/Baharna" class="mw-redirect" title="Baharna">Baharna</a>, probably because they share the same Shia Muslim faith, however, some Sunni Persians settled in areas mostly inhabited by Sunni Arab immigrants such as <a href="/wiki/Hidd" class="mw-redirect" title="Hidd">Hidd</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galali,_Bahrain" title="Galali, Bahrain">Galali</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Muharraq" title="Muharraq">Muharraq</a>, they have their own neighborhood called Fareej Karimi named after a rich Persian man called Ali Abdulla Karimi.
</p><p>From the 6th century BC to the 3rd century BC, Bahrain was a prominent part of the Persian Empire by the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenids</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian dynasty</a>. Bahrain was referred to by the Greeks as "<a href="/wiki/Tylos" title="Tylos">Tylos</a>", the centre of pearl trading, when <a href="/wiki/Nearchus" title="Nearchus">Nearchus</a> discovered it while serving under <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Larsen_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Larsen-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> From the 3rd century BC to the arrival of Islam in the 7th century AD, Bahrain was controlled by two other Iranian dynasties, the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sassanids" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanids">Sassanids</a>.
</p><p>In the 3rd century AD, the Sassanids succeeded the Parthians and controlled the area for four centuries until the arrival of Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-Federal_Research_Division_page_7_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Federal_Research_Division_page_7-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ardashir_I" title="Ardashir I">Ardashir</a>, the first ruler of the Iranian Sassanid dynasty marched to Oman and Bahrain and defeated Sanatruq<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> (or Satiran<sup id="cite_ref-Mojtahed-Zadeh_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mojtahed-Zadeh-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>), probably the Parthian governor of Bahrain.<sup id="cite_ref-Jamsheed_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jamsheed-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> He appointed his son <a href="/wiki/Shapur_I" title="Shapur I">Shapur I</a> as governor of Bahrain. Shapur constructed a new city there and named it Batan Ardashir after his father.<sup id="cite_ref-Mojtahed-Zadeh_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mojtahed-Zadeh-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> At this time, Bahrain incorporated the southern Sassanid province covering the Persian Gulf's southern shore plus the archipelago of Bahrain.<sup id="cite_ref-Jamsheed_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jamsheed-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The southern province of the Sassanids was subdivided into three districts; Haggar (now al-Hafuf province, Saudi Arabia), Batan Ardashir (now <a href="/wiki/Al-Qatif" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Qatif">al-Qatif</a> province, Saudi Arabia), and <a href="/wiki/Mishmahig" class="mw-redirect" title="Mishmahig">Mishmahig</a> (now Bahrain Island)<sup id="cite_ref-Mojtahed-Zadeh_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mojtahed-Zadeh-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> (In  <a href="/wiki/Middle-Persian" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle-Persian">Middle-Persian</a>/Pahlavi it means "ewe-fish").<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By about 130 BC, the Parthian dynasty brought the Persian Gulf under their control and extended their influence as far as Oman. Because they needed to control the Persian Gulf trade route, the Parthians established garrisons along the southern coast of the Persian Gulf.<sup id="cite_ref-Federal_Research_Division_page_7_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Federal_Research_Division_page_7-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
through warfare and economic distress, been reduced to only 60.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> 
The influence of Iran was further undermined at the end of the 18th century when the ideological power struggle between the Akhbari-Usuli strands culminated in victory for the Usulis in Bahrain.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An Afghan uprising led by Hotakis of Kandahar at the beginning of the 18th century resulted in the near collapse of the Safavid state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBashir19797_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBashir19797-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In the resultant power vacuum, <a href="/wiki/1717_Omani_invasion_of_Bahrain" title="1717 Omani invasion of Bahrain">Oman invaded Bahrain in 1717</a>, ending over one hundred years of Persian hegemony in Bahrain. The Omani invasion began a period of political instability and a quick succession of outside rulers took power with consequent destruction. According to a contemporary account by theologian, Sheikh Yusuf Al Bahrani, in an unsuccessful attempt by the Persians and their Bedouin allies to take back Bahrain from the <a href="/wiki/Kharijite" class="mw-redirect" title="Kharijite">Kharijite</a> Omanis, much of the country was burnt to the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Bahrain was eventually sold back to the Persians by the Omanis, but the weakness of the Safavid empire saw <a href="/wiki/Huwala" class="mw-redirect" title="Huwala">Huwala</a> tribes seize control.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1730, the new Shah of <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nadir_Shah" class="mw-redirect" title="Nadir Shah">Nadir Shah</a>, sought to re-assert Persian sovereignty in Bahrain. He ordered Latif Khan, the admiral of the Persian navy in the Persian Gulf, to prepare an invasion fleet in <a href="/wiki/Bushehr" title="Bushehr">Bushehr</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBashir19797_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBashir19797-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The Persians invaded in March or early April 1736 when the ruler of Bahrain, Shaikh Jubayr, was away on <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">hajj</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBashir19797_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBashir19797-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The invasion brought the island back under central rule and to challenge Oman in the Persian Gulf. He sought help from the British and Dutch, and he eventually recaptured Bahrain in 1736.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Qajar" class="mw-redirect" title="Qajar">Qajar</a> era, Persian control over Bahrain waned<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBashir19797_52-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBashir19797-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and in 1753, Bahrain was occupied by the Sunni Persians of the <a href="/wiki/Bushire" class="mw-redirect" title="Bushire">Bushire</a>-based Al Madhkur family,<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> who ruled Bahrain in the name of Persia and paid allegiance to <a href="/wiki/Karim_Khan_Zand" title="Karim Khan Zand">Karim Khan Zand</a>.
</p><p>During most of the second half of the eighteenth century, Bahrain was ruled by <a href="/wiki/Nasr_Al-Madhkur" title="Nasr Al-Madhkur">Nasr Al-Madhkur</a>, the ruler of <a href="/wiki/Bushehr" title="Bushehr">Bushehr</a>. The Bani Utibah tribe from Zubarah exceeded in taking over Bahrain after a war broke out in 1782. Persian attempts to reconquer the island in 1783 and in 1785 failed; the 1783 expedition was a joint Persian-<a href="/wiki/Qawasim" class="mw-redirect" title="Qawasim">Qawasim</a> invasion force that never left Bushehr. The 1785 invasion fleet, composed of forces from Bushehr, Rig and <a href="/wiki/Shiraz" title="Shiraz">Shiraz</a> was called off after the death of the ruler of Shiraz, <a href="/wiki/Ali_Murad_Khan" class="mw-redirect" title="Ali Murad Khan">Ali Murad Khan</a>. Due to internal difficulties, the Persians could not attempt another invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBashir197946_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBashir197946-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In 1799, Bahrain came under threat from the <a href="/wiki/Expansionist" class="mw-redirect" title="Expansionist">expansionist</a> policies of <a href="/wiki/Said_bin_Sultan,_Sultan_of_Muscat_and_Oman" title="Said bin Sultan, Sultan of Muscat and Oman">Sayyid Sultan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sultan_of_Oman" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultan of Oman">Sultan of Oman</a>, when he invaded the island under the pretext that Bahrain did not pay taxes owed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBashir197947_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBashir197947-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The Bani Utbah solicited the aid of Bushire to expel the Omanis on the condition that Bahrain would become a <a href="/wiki/Tributary_state" title="Tributary state">tributary state</a> of Persia. In 1800, Sayyid Sultan invaded Bahrain again in retaliation and deployed a garrison at <a href="/wiki/Arad_Fort" title="Arad Fort">Arad Fort</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Muharraq" title="Muharraq">Muharraq</a> island and had appointed his twelve-year-old son Salim, as Governor of the island.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBashir197947_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBashir197947-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many names of villages in Bahrain are derived from the <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> language.<sup id="cite_ref-Tajer_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tajer-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> These names were thought to have been as a result influences during the <a href="/wiki/Safavid" class="mw-redirect" title="Safavid">Safavid</a> rule of Bahrain (1501–1722) and previous Persian rule. Village names such as <a href="/wiki/Karbabad" title="Karbabad">Karbabad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salmabad" title="Salmabad">Salmabad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karzakan" title="Karzakan">Karzakan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duraz" class="mw-redirect" title="Duraz">Duraz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barbar,_Bahrain" title="Barbar, Bahrain">Barbar</a> were originally derived from the Persian language, suggesting that Persians had a substantial effect on the island's history.<sup id="cite_ref-Tajer_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tajer-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The local <a href="/wiki/Bahrani_Arabic" title="Bahrani Arabic">Bahrani Arabic</a> dialect has also borrowed many words from the Persian language.<sup id="cite_ref-Tajer_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tajer-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Bahrain's capital city, <a href="/wiki/Manama" title="Manama">Manama</a> is derived from two Persian words meaning 'I' and 'speech'.<sup id="cite_ref-Tajer_60-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tajer-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Manama" title="Manama"><span title="This text contradicts material at the article &#39;Manama&#39;, section &#39;Etymology&#39;. (March 2018)">contradictory</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In 1910, the Persian community funded and opened a <a href="/wiki/Private_school" title="Private school">private school</a>, Al-Ittihad school, that taught <a href="/wiki/Farsi" class="mw-redirect" title="Farsi">Farsi</a> amongst other subjects.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
According to the 1905 census, there were 1650 Bahraini citizens of Persian origin.<sup id="cite_ref-pol_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pol-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historian Nasser Hussain says that many Iranians fled their native country in the early 20th century due to a law king <a href="/wiki/Reza_Shah" title="Reza Shah">Reza Shah</a> issued which banned women from wearing the <a href="/wiki/Hijab" title="Hijab">hijab</a>, or because they feared for their lives after fighting the English, or to find jobs. They were coming to Bahrain from Bushehr and the <a href="/wiki/Fars_province" class="mw-redirect" title="Fars province">Fars province</a> between 1920 and 1940. In the 1920s, local Persian merchants were prominently involved in the consolidation of Bahrain's first powerful lobby with connections to the municipality in effort to contest the municipal legislation of British control.<sup id="cite_ref-pol_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pol-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bahrain's local Persian community have heavily influenced the country's local food dishes. One of the most notable local delicacies of the people in Bahrain is <a href="/wiki/Mahyawa" title="Mahyawa">mahyawa</a>, consumed in Southern Iran as well, is a watery earth brick coloured sauce made from sardines and consumed with bread or other food. Bahrain's Persians are also famous in Bahrain for bread-making. Another local delicacy is "pishoo" made from rose water (golab) and agar agar. Other food items consumed are similar to <a href="/wiki/Persian_cuisine" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian cuisine">Persian cuisine</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Iraq">Iraq</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Iraq">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_relations" title="Iran–Iraq relations">Iran–Iraq relations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_War" title="Iran–Iraq War">Iran–Iraq War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persians_in_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Persians in Iraq">Persians in Iraq</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Asuristan" class="mw-redirect" title="Asuristan">Asuristan</a></div>
<p>Throughout history, Iran always had strong cultural ties with the region of present–day <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>. Mesopotamia is considered as the cradle of civilization and the place where the first empires in history were established. These empires, namely the <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian</a>, dominated the ancient middle east for millennia, which explains the great influence of the Mesopotamia on the Iranian culture and history, and it is also the reason why the later Iranian and Greek dynasties chose Mesopotamia to be the political centre of their rule. For a period of around 500 years, what is now Iraq formed the core of Iran, with the Iranian <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian</a> empire having their capital in what is modern-day Iraq for the same centuries long time span. (<a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a>)
</p>
<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“
</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">Of the four residences of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenids</a> named by <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>—<a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">Ecbatana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pasargadae" title="Pasargadae">Pasargadae</a> or <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>—the last [situated in Iraq] was maintained as their most important capital, the fixed winter quarters, the central office of bureaucracy, exchanged only in the heat of summer for some cool spot in the highlands.<sup id="cite_ref-EY_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EY-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
<p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucids</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a> the site of the Mesopotamian capital moved a little to the north on the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>—to <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a>. It is indeed symbolic that these new foundations were built from the bricks of ancient <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>, just as later <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, a little further upstream, was built out of the ruins of the <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanian</a> double city of <a href="/wiki/Al-Mada%27in" title="Al-Mada&#39;in">Seleucia-Ctesiphon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EY_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EY-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”
</td></tr>
