<td colspan="3" class="cquotecite" style="border: none; padding-right: 4%; font-size: smaller; line-height:normal; text-align: right;"><cite>—&#8202;Iranologist <a href="/wiki/Ehsan_Yarshater" title="Ehsan Yarshater">Ehsan Yarshater</a>, The Cambridge History of Iran,<sup id="cite_ref-EY_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EY-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cyrus_Cylinder_front.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Cyrus_Cylinder_front.jpg/300px-Cyrus_Cylinder_front.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Cyrus_Cylinder_front.jpg/450px-Cyrus_Cylinder_front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Cyrus_Cylinder_front.jpg/600px-Cyrus_Cylinder_front.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2968" data-file-height="1788" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cyrus_Cylinder_front.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_Cylinder" title="Cyrus Cylinder">Cyrus Cylinder</a>, written in <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Babylonian</a> <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform</a> in the name of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid</a> king, <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a>, describes the Persian takeover of <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylon</a> (An ancient city in modern-day Iraq).</div></div></div>
<p>Because the Achaemenid Empire or "First Persian Empire" was the successor state to the empires of <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Assyria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">Babylonia</a> based in Iraq, and because <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a> is part of Iran, the ancient people of Iran were ruled by ancient Mesopotamians, which explains the close proximity between the people of south western Iran and the Iraqis even in modern days, in fact, the people of that part of Iran speak Mesopotamian Arabic and were put under the rule of modern Iran by the British. The ancient Persians adopted the Babylonian <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform script</a> and <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian_cuneiform" title="Old Persian cuneiform">modified it</a> to write their <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Persian language">language</a>, along with adopting many other facets of ancient Iraqi culture, including the <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic language</a> which became the official language of the Persian Empire.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_Cylinder" title="Cyrus Cylinder">Cyrus Cylinder</a>, written in Babylonian cuneiform in the name of the Achaemenid king <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a>, describes the Persian takeover of Babylon (the ancient name of Iraq). An excerpt reads:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>When I entered Babylon in a peaceful manner, I took up my lordly abode in the royal palace amidst rejoicing and happiness. <a href="/wiki/Marduk" title="Marduk">Marduk</a>, the great lord, established as his fate for me a <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/magnanimous" class="extiw" title="wikt:magnanimous">magnanimous</a> heart of one who loves Babylon, and I daily attended to his worship. My vast army marched into Babylon in peace; I did not permit anyone to frighten the people of <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Sumer and Akkad</a>. I sought the welfare of the city of Babylon and all its sacred centers. As for the citizens of Babylon,[...] upon whom <a href="/wiki/Nabonidus" title="Nabonidus">Nabonidus</a> imposed a <a href="/wiki/Corv%C3%A9e" title="Corvée">corvée</a> which was not the gods' wish and not befitting them, I relieved their wariness and freed them from their service. Marduk, the great lord, rejoiced over my good deeds. He sent gracious blessing upon me, <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus</a>, the king who worships him, and upon <a href="/wiki/Cambyses_II" title="Cambyses II">Cambyses</a>, the son who is my offspring, and upon all my army, and in peace, before him, we moved around in friendship [with the people of Babylon].</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Cyrus Cylinder</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Persia_1814.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Persia_1814.jpg/300px-Persia_1814.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="251" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Persia_1814.jpg/450px-Persia_1814.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Persia_1814.jpg/600px-Persia_1814.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2931" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Persia_1814.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 1814 map of <a href="/wiki/Qajar_dynasty" title="Qajar dynasty">Persia</a> at time of <a href="/wiki/Qajar_dynasty" title="Qajar dynasty">Qajar dynasty</a></div></div></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Iranian_studies" title="Iranian studies">Iranologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Richard_N._Frye" title="Richard N. Frye">Richard N. Frye</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NY_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NY-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Throughout Iran's history the western part of the land has been frequently more closely connected with the <a href="/wiki/Lowland" class="mw-redirect" title="Lowland">lowlands</a> of Mesopotamia (Iraq) than with the rest of the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_plateau" class="mw-redirect" title="Iranian plateau">plateau</a> to the east of the central deserts [the <a href="/wiki/Dasht-e_Kavir" title="Dasht-e Kavir">Dasht-e Kavir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dasht-e_Lut" title="Dasht-e Lut">Dasht-e Lut</a>].</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Richard N. Frye, <i>The Golden Age of Persia: The Arabs in the East</i></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<table class="rquote pullquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">

<tbody><tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“
</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;">Between the coming of the Abbasids [in 750] and the Mongol onslaught [in 1258], Iraq and western Iran shared a closer history than did eastern Iran and its western counterpart.
</td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:normal; text-align:right;"><cite>—&#160; Neguin Yavari ,&#32; <i>Iranian Perspectives on the Iran–Iraq War</i><sup id="cite_ref-NY_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NY-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Testimony to the close relationship shared by Iraq and western Iran during the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid era</a> and later centuries, is the fact that the two regions came to share the same name. The western region of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> (ancient Media) was called <a href="/wiki/Persian_Iraq" title="Persian Iraq">'Irāq-e 'Ajamī</a> ("Persian Iraq"), while central-southern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> (Babylonia) was called 'Irāq al-'Arabī ("Arabic Iraq") or Bābil ("Babylon"). And the name Iraq comes from the ancient Mesopotamian city Uruk, which suggests an even older relationship.
</p><p>For centuries the two neighbouring regions were known as "<a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Two_Iraqs&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="The Two Iraqs (page does not exist)">The Two Iraqs</a>" ("al-'Iraqain"). The 12th century Persian poet <a href="/wiki/Khaqani" title="Khaqani">Khāqāni</a> wrote a famous poem <i>Tohfat-ul Iraqein</i> ("The Gift of the Two Iraqs"). The city of <a href="/wiki/Arak,_Iran" title="Arak, Iran">Arāk</a> in western Iran still bears the region's old name, and Iranians still traditionally call the region between <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">Tehran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isfahan" title="Isfahan">Isfahan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ilam,_Iran" title="Ilam, Iran">Īlām</a> "ʿErāq".
</p><p>During medieval ages, Mesopotamian and Iranian peoples knew each other's languages because of trade, and because Arabic was the language of religion and science at that time. The <a href="/wiki/Timurid_dynasty" title="Timurid dynasty">Timurid</a> historian <a href="/wiki/%E1%B8%A4%C4%81fe%E1%BA%93-e_Abru" class="mw-redirect" title="Ḥāfeẓ-e Abru">Ḥāfeẓ-e Abru</a> (d. 1430) wrote of Iraq:<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The majority of inhabitants of Iraq know <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, and from the time of domination of <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic people</a> the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish language</a> has also found currency.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Ḥāfeẓ-e Abru</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Iraqi_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi people">Iraqis</a> share religious and certain cultural ties with <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Iran" title="Demographics of Iran">Iranians</a>. The majority of Iranians are Twelver <a href="/wiki/Shia" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia">Shia</a> (an Islamic sect established in Iraq), although the majority of Iranians were <a href="/wiki/Sunni" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> Muslims and did not convert to Shia until the <a href="/wiki/Safavids" class="mw-redirect" title="Safavids">Safavids</a> forced <a href="/wiki/Shi%27ism" class="mw-redirect" title="Shi&#39;ism">Shi'ism</a> in Iran.
</p><p>Iraqi culture has commonalities with the <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Iran" title="Culture of Iran">culture of Iran</a>. The spring festival of <a href="/wiki/Nowruz" title="Nowruz">Nowruz</a> that is celebrated in Iran and some parts of Iraq roots back to the Akitu spring festival (Babylonian new year). The <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_cuisine" title="Iraqi cuisine">Mesopotamian cuisine</a> also has similarities to the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_cuisine" title="Iranian cuisine">Persian cuisine</a>, including common dishes and cooking techniques. The <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Arabic" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi Arabic">Iraqi dialect</a> has absorbed many words from the <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are still cities and provinces in Iraq where the Persian names of the city are still retained – e.g., <a href="/wiki/Al_Anbar_Governorate" title="Al Anbar Governorate">’Anbār</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>. Other cities of Iraq with originally Persian names include <i>Nokard</i> (نوكرد) --&gt; <a href="/wiki/Hdatta" title="Hdatta">Haditha</a>,  <i><a href="/wiki/Suristan" title="Suristan">Suristan</a></i> (سورستان) --&gt; <a href="/wiki/Kufa" title="Kufa">Kufa</a>, <i>Shahrban</i> (شهربان) --&gt; <a href="/wiki/Muqdadiyah" class="mw-redirect" title="Muqdadiyah">Muqdadiyah</a>, <i>Arvandrud</i> (اروندرود) --&gt; <a href="/wiki/Shatt_al-Arab" title="Shatt al-Arab">Shatt al-Arab</a>, and <i>Asheb</i> (آشب) --&gt; <a href="/wiki/Amadiya" title="Amadiya">Amadiya</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> <i>Peroz-Shapur</i> --&gt; <a href="/wiki/Anbar_(town)" title="Anbar (town)">Anbar (town)</a>
</p><p>In the modern era, the <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavid dynasty</a> of Iran briefly reasserted hegemony over Iraq in the periods of <a href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Safavid_War_(1532%E2%80%931555)" title="Ottoman–Safavid War (1532–1555)">1501–1533</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Safavid_War_(1623%E2%80%931639)" title="Ottoman–Safavid War (1623–1639)">1622–1638</a>, losing Iraq to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> on both occasions (via the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Amasya" title="Peace of Amasya">Treaty of Amasya</a> in 1555 and the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Zuhab" title="Treaty of Zuhab">Treaty of Zuhab</a> in 1639). Ottoman hegemony over Iraq was reconfirmed in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Kerden" title="Treaty of Kerden">Treaty of Kerden</a> in 1746.
</p><p>Following the fall of the Ba'athist regime in 2003 and the empowerment of Iraq's majority Shī'i community, relations with Iran have flourished in all fields. Iraq is today Iran's largest trading partner in regard to non-oil goods.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many Iranians were born in Iraq or have ancestors from Iraq,<sup id="cite_ref-MN_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MN-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> such as the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Consultative_Assembly" title="Islamic Consultative Assembly">Chairman of Iran's Parliament</a> <a href="/wiki/Ali_Larijani" title="Ali Larijani">Ali Larijani</a>, the former <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_Iran" title="Chief Justice of Iran">Chief Justice of Iran</a> <a href="/wiki/Mahmoud_Hashemi_Shahroudi" title="Mahmoud Hashemi Shahroudi">Mahmoud Hashemi Shahroudi</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Affairs_Minister_of_Iran" class="mw-redirect" title="Foreign Affairs Minister of Iran">Minister of Foreign Affairs of Iran</a> <a href="/wiki/Ali_Akbar_Salehi" title="Ali Akbar Salehi">Ali Akbar Salehi</a>, who were born in <a href="/wiki/Najaf" title="Najaf">Najaf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karbala" title="Karbala">Karbala</a> respectively. In the same way, many Iraqis were born in Iran or have ancestors from Iran,<sup id="cite_ref-MN_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MN-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Grand_Ayatollah" class="mw-redirect" title="Grand Ayatollah">Grand Ayatollah</a> <a href="/wiki/Ali_al-Sistani" title="Ali al-Sistani">Ali al-Sistani</a>, who was born in <a href="/wiki/Mashhad" title="Mashhad">Mashhad</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kurdistan">Kurdistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Kurdistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Culturally and historically <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan" title="Kurdistan">Kurdistan</a> has been a part of what is known as Greater Iran. <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish people">Kurds</a> speak a Northwestern Iranian language known as <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish language">Kurdish</a>. Many aspects of <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_culture" title="Kurdish culture">Kurdish culture</a> are related to the other peoples of Greater Iran, examples include <a href="/wiki/Newroz_as_celebrated_by_Kurds" title="Newroz as celebrated by Kurds">Newroz</a><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Simurgh" title="Simurgh">Simurgh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Iranica_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iranica-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Some historians and linguists, such as <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Minorsky" title="Vladimir Minorsky">Vladimir Minorsky</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> have suggested that the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a>, an Iranian people<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> who inhabited much of western Iran, including Azerbaijan and Kurdistan, might have been forefathers of modern Kurds.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Caucasus">Caucasus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Caucasus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Caucasus">North Caucasus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: North Caucasus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Derbent_winter.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Derbent_winter.jpg/220px-Derbent_winter.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Derbent_winter.jpg/330px-Derbent_winter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Derbent_winter.jpg/440px-Derbent_winter.jpg 2x" data-file-width="557" data-file-height="393" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Derbent_winter.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sassanian" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanian">Sassanian</a> fortress in <a href="/wiki/Derbent" title="Derbent">Derbent</a>, Dagestan. Now inscribed on Russia's <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> world heritage list since 2003.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Dagestan" title="History of Dagestan">History of Dagestan</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Kabardino-Balkaria" title="History of Kabardino-Balkaria">History of Kabardino-Balkaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russo-Persian_Wars" title="Russo-Persian Wars">Russo-Persian Wars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Gulistan" title="Treaty of Gulistan">Treaty of Gulistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Turkmenchay" title="Treaty of Turkmenchay">Treaty of Turkmenchay</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tat_people_(Caucasus)" title="Tat people (Caucasus)">Tat people (Caucasus)</a></div>
<p>Dagestan remains the bastion of <a href="/wiki/Persian_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian culture">Persian culture</a> in the <a href="/wiki/North_Caucasus" title="North Caucasus">North Caucasus</a> with fine examples of Iranian architecture like the Sassanid citadel in <a href="/wiki/Derbent" title="Derbent">Derbent</a>, strong influence of <a href="/wiki/Persian_cuisine" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian cuisine">Persian cuisine</a>, and common Persian names amongst the ethnic peoples of Dagestan. The ethnic Persian population of the North Caucasus, the <a href="/wiki/Tat_people_(Caucasus)" title="Tat people (Caucasus)">Tats</a>, remain, despite strong assimilation over the years, still visible in several North Caucasian cities. Even today, after decades of partition, some of these regions retain Iranian influences, as seen in their old beliefs, traditions and customs (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Nowruz" title="Nowruz">Norouz</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Caucasus">South Caucasus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: South Caucasus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Azerbaijani people">Azerbaijani people</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Azerbaijan" title="History of Azerbaijan">History of Azerbaijan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tat_people_(Iran)" title="Tat people (Iran)">Tat people (Iran)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tat_people_(Caucasus)" title="Tat people (Caucasus)">Tat people (Caucasus)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Safavid_conversion_of_Iran_to_Shia_Islam" title="Safavid conversion of Iran to Shia Islam">Safavid conversion of Iran to Shia Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Azeri_language" title="Old Azeri language">Old Azeri language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shirvan" title="Shirvan">Shirvan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arran_(Caucasus)" title="Arran (Caucasus)">Arran (Caucasus)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shirvanshah" title="Shirvanshah">Shirvanshah</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Azerbaijanis" title="Iranian Azerbaijanis">Iranian Azerbaijanis</a></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Tadeusz_Swietochowski" title="Tadeusz Swietochowski">Tadeusz Swietochowski</a>, the territories of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and the republic of <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> usually shared the same history from the time of ancient Media (ninth to seventh centuries b.c.) and the Persian Empire (sixth to fourth centuries b.c.).<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Intimately and inseparably intertwined histories for millennia, Iran irrevocably lost the territory that is nowadays Azerbaijan in the course of the 19th century. With the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Gulistan" title="Treaty of Gulistan">Treaty of Gulistan</a> of 1813 following the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Persian_War_(1804-1813)" class="mw-redirect" title="Russo-Persian War (1804-1813)">Russo-Persian War (1804-1813)</a> Iran had to cede eastern <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, its possessions in the <a href="/wiki/North_Caucasus" title="North Caucasus">North Caucasus</a> and many of those in what is today the <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan Republic</a>, which included <a href="/wiki/Baku_Khanate" title="Baku Khanate">Baku Khanate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shirvan_Khanate" title="Shirvan Khanate">Shirvan Khanate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karabakh_Khanate" title="Karabakh Khanate">Karabakh Khanate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganja_Khanate" title="Ganja Khanate">Ganja Khanate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shaki_Khanate" title="Shaki Khanate">Shaki Khanate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quba_Khanate" title="Quba Khanate">Quba Khanate</a>, and parts of the <a href="/wiki/Talysh_Khanate" title="Talysh Khanate">Talysh Khanate</a>. <a href="/wiki/Derbent" title="Derbent">Derbent</a> (Darband) <a href="/wiki/Derbent_khanate" class="mw-redirect" title="Derbent khanate">Khanate</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dagestan" title="Dagestan">Dagestan</a> was also lost to Russia. These Khanates comprise most of what is today the Republic of Azerbaijan and Dagestan in Southern Russia. By the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Turkmenchay" title="Treaty of Turkmenchay">Treaty of Turkmenchay</a> of 1828 following the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Persian_War_(1826-1828)" class="mw-redirect" title="Russo-Persian War (1826-1828)">Russo-Persian War (1826-1828)</a>, the result was even more disastrous, and resulted in Iran being forced to cede the <a href="/wiki/Nakhichevan_Khanate" title="Nakhichevan Khanate">Nakhichevan Khanate</a> and the Mughan regions to Russia, as well as <a href="/wiki/Erivan_Khanate" title="Erivan Khanate">Erivan Khanate</a>, and the remainder of the Talysh Khanate. All these territories together, lost in 1813 and 1828 combined, constitute all of the modern-day Republic of Azerbaijan, <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, and southern <a href="/wiki/Dagestan" title="Dagestan">Dagestan</a>. The area to the North of the river <a href="/wiki/Aras_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Aras River">Aras</a>, among which the territory of the contemporary republic of Azerbaijan were Iranian territory until they were occupied by Russia in the course of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Swietochowski_Borderland_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swietochowski_Borderland-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many localities in this region bear Persian names or names derived from Iranian languages and Azerbaijan remains by far Iran's closest cultural, religious, ethnic and historical neighbor. <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijanis" title="Azerbaijanis">Azerbaijanis</a> are by far the second largest ethnicity in Iran, and comprise the largest community of ethnic Azerbaijanis in the world, vastly outnumbering the number in the Republic of Azerbaijan. Both nations are the only officially Shia majority in the world, with adherents of the religion comprising an absolute majority in both nations. The people of nowadays Iran and Azerbaijan were <a href="/wiki/Safavid_conversion_of_Iran_to_Shia_Islam" title="Safavid conversion of Iran to Shia Islam">converted to Shiism</a> during exactly the same time in history. Furthermore, the name of "Azerbaijan" is derived through the name of the Persian <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a> which ruled the contemporary region of <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Azerbaijan" class="mw-redirect" title="Iranian Azerbaijan">Iranian Azerbaijan</a> and minor parts of the Republic of Azerbaijan in ancient times.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schippmann_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schippmann-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In 1918, the Azerbaijani <a href="/wiki/Musavat" title="Musavat">Musavat</a> party adopted the name for the nation upon the independence of the former territories under the Russian Empire.
</p><p>Early in antiquity, <a href="/wiki/Narseh_of_Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Narseh of Persia">Narseh of Persia</a> is known to have had fortifications built here. In later times, some of Persia's literary and intellectual figures from the <a href="/wiki/Qajar_dynasty" title="Qajar dynasty">Qajar</a> period have hailed from this region. Under intermittent Iranian suzerainty since antiquity, it was also separated from Iran in the mid-19th century, by virtue of the Gulistan Treaty and Turkmenchay Treaty.
</p><p>که تا جایگه یافتی نخچوان<br />
Oh <a href="/wiki/Nakhichivan_Autonomous_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Nakhichivan Autonomous Republic">Nakhchivan</a>, respect you've attained,<br />
بدین شاه شد بخت پیرت جوان<br />
With this King in luck you'll remain.<br />
<i>---<a href="/wiki/Nizami_Ganjavi" title="Nizami Ganjavi">Nizami</a></i>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Asia">Central Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Central Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BactrianZoroastrian.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/BactrianZoroastrian.jpg/250px-BactrianZoroastrian.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="338" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/BactrianZoroastrian.jpg/375px-BactrianZoroastrian.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/53/BactrianZoroastrian.jpg 2x" data-file-width="475" data-file-height="643" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BactrianZoroastrian.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Painted clay and <a href="/wiki/Alabaster" title="Alabaster">alabaster</a> head of a <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Zoroastrian">Zoroastrian</a> priest wearing a distinctive <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactrian</a>-style headdress, <a href="/wiki/Takhti-Sangin" title="Takhti-Sangin">Takhti-Sangin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Greco-Bactrian kingdom">Greco-Bactrian kingdom</a>, 3rd-2nd century BC</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Khwarazm" title="Khwarazm">Khwarazm</a> is one of the regions of <i>Iran-zameen</i>, and is the home of the ancient Iranians, <a href="/wiki/Airyanem_Vaejah" title="Airyanem Vaejah">Airyanem Vaejah</a>, according to the ancient book of the <a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">Avesta</a>. Modern scholars believe Khwarazm to be what ancient Avestic texts refer to as "Ariyaneh Waeje" or Iran vij. <i>Iranovich</i>  These sources claim that <a href="/wiki/Kunya_Urgench" class="mw-redirect" title="Kunya Urgench">Urgandj</a>, which was the capital of ancient Khwarazm for many years, was actually "Ourva": the eighth land of <a href="/wiki/Ahura_Mazda" title="Ahura Mazda">Ahura Mazda</a> mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Pahlavi_scripts" title="Pahlavi scripts">Pahlavi</a> text of Vendidad. Others such as <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii" title="University of Hawaii">University of Hawaii</a> historian <a href="/wiki/Elton_L._Daniel" title="Elton L. Daniel">Elton L. Daniel</a> believe Khwarazm to be the "most likely locale" corresponding to the original home of the Avestan people,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> while Dehkhoda calls Khwarazm "the cradle of the <a href="/wiki/Arya" class="mw-redirect" title="Arya">Aryan</a> tribe" (مهد قوم آریا). Today Khwarazm is split between several central Asian republics.
</p><p>Superimposed on and overlapping with Chorasmia was Khorasan which roughly covered nearly the same geographical areas in Central Asia (starting from <a href="/wiki/Semnan_(city)" class="mw-redirect" title="Semnan (city)">Semnan</a> eastward through northern Afghanistan roughly until the foothills of <a href="/wiki/Pamir_Mountains" title="Pamir Mountains">Pamir</a>, ancient <a href="/wiki/Mount_Imeon" title="Mount Imeon">Mount Imeon</a>). Current day provinces such as <a href="/wiki/Sanjan_(Khorasan)" title="Sanjan (Khorasan)">Sanjan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Razavi_Khorasan_Province" title="Razavi Khorasan Province">Razavi Khorasan Province</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Khorasan_Province" title="North Khorasan Province">North Khorasan Province</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Khorasan_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern Khorasan Province">Southern Khorasan Province</a> in Iran are all remnants of the old Khorasan. Until the 13th century and the devastating Mongol invasion of the region, Khorasan was considered the cultural capital of Greater Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Tajikistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The national anthem in Tajikistan, "<a href="/wiki/Surudi_Milli" title="Surudi Milli">Surudi Milli</a>", attests to the Perso-Tajik identity, which has seen a large revival, after the breakup of the <a href="/wiki/USSR" class="mw-redirect" title="USSR">USSR</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tajik_language" title="Tajik language">Their language</a> is almost identical to that spoken in Afghanistan and Iran, and their cities have Persian names, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Dushanbe" title="Dushanbe">Dushanbe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isfara" title="Isfara">Isfara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rasht_Valley" title="Rasht Valley">Rasht Valley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Garm,_Tajikistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Garm, Tajikistan">Garm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Murghab_River_(Tajikistan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Murghab River (Tajikistan)">Murghab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vahdat" title="Vahdat">Vahdat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zeravshan" class="mw-redirect" title="Zeravshan">Zar-afshan river</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shurab,_Tajikistan" title="Shurab, Tajikistan">Shurab</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kulob" title="Kulob">Kulob</a> (<a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.iranicaonline.org/newsite/articles/ot_grp8/ot_kulab_20050727.html">[2]</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title="&#160;Dead link since January 2018">permanent dead link</span></a></i>&#93;</span></sup>). It is also important<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material in the vicinity of this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (December 2015)">to whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> to note that <a href="/wiki/Rudaki" title="Rudaki">Rudaki</a>, considered by many as the father of modern Persian poetry, was from the modern day region of Tajikistan.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Turkmenistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Home of the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nisa,_Turkmenistan" title="Nisa, Turkmenistan">Nysa</a>). Merv is also where the half-Persian caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Mamun" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Mamun">al-Mamun</a> moved his capital to. The city of <a href="/wiki/Ashgabat" title="Ashgabat">Eshgh Abad</a> (some claim that the word is actually the transformed form of "Ashk Abad" literally meaning "built by Ashk", the head of Arsacid dynasty) is yet another Persian word meaning "city of love", and like Iran, Afghanistan, and Uzbekistan, it was once part of <a href="/wiki/Airyanem_Vaejah" title="Airyanem Vaejah">Airyanem Vaejah</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Uzbekistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Uzbekistan has a local Tajik population. The famous Persian cities of <a href="/wiki/Afrasiab" title="Afrasiab">Afrasiab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shahrisabz" title="Shahrisabz">Shahrisabz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andijan" title="Andijan">Andijan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khiva" title="Khiva">Khiveh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Navoiy" title="Navoiy">Navā'i</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shirin" title="Shirin">Shirin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Termez" title="Termez">Termez</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zarafshan" title="Zarafshan">Zar-afshan</a> are located here. These cities are the birthplace of the Islamic era Persian literature. The <a href="/wiki/Samanid" class="mw-redirect" title="Samanid">Samanids</a>, who claimed inheritance to the Sassanids, had their capital built here.
</p><p>ای بخارا شاد باش و دیر زی<br />
Oh Bukhara! Joy to you and live long!<br />
شاه زی تو میهمان آید همی<br />
Your King comes to you in ceremony.<br />
<i>---<a href="/wiki/Rudaki" title="Rudaki">Rudaki</a></i>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Xinjiang">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="box-Synthesis plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Ambox_question.svg" class="image"><img alt="Ambox question.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Ambox_question.svg/40px-Ambox_question.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Ambox_question.svg/60px-Ambox_question.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Ambox_question.svg/80px-Ambox_question.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article or section <b>possibly contains <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Synthesis_of_published_material" title="Wikipedia:No original research">synthesis of material</a> which does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verifiably mention</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Content_removal#Irrelevant_information" title="Wikipedia:Content removal">relate</a> to the main topic.</b><span class="hide-when-compact"> Relevant discussion may be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Greater_Iran##" title="Talk:Greater Iran">talk page</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2015</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Iran-China_relations" class="mw-redirect" title="Iran-China relations">Iran-China relations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tajiks_in_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Tajiks in China">Tajiks in China</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tashkurgan_Tajik_Autonomous_County" class="mw-redirect" title="Tashkurgan Tajik Autonomous County">Tashkurgan Tajik Autonomous County</a> regions of China harbored a Tajik population and culture.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Chinese Tashkurgan Tajik Autonomous County was always counted as a part of the Iranian cultural &amp; linguistic continent with <a href="/wiki/Kashgar" title="Kashgar">Kashgar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yarkant_County" title="Yarkant County">Yarkand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hotan" title="Hotan">Hotan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Turpan" title="Turpan">Turpan</a> bound to the Iranian history.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Asia">South Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: South Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Afghanistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Modern state of Afghanistan was part of <a href="/wiki/Sistan" title="Sistan">Sistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Greater Khorasan</a> regions, and hence was recognized with the name Khorasan (along with regions centered on Merv and Nishapur), which in Pahlavi means "The Eastern Land" (خاور زمین in Persian).<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nowadays region of Afghanistan is where <a href="/wiki/Balkh" title="Balkh">Balkh</a> is located, home of <a href="/wiki/Rumi" title="Rumi">Rumi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rabi%27a_Balkhi" class="mw-redirect" title="Rabi&#39;a Balkhi">Rabi'a Balkhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sanai" title="Sanai">Sanāī Ghaznawi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jami" title="Jami">Jami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khwaja_Abdullah_Ansari" title="Khwaja Abdullah Ansari">Khwaja Abdullah Ansari</a> and where many other notables in <a href="/wiki/Persian_literature" title="Persian literature">Persian literature</a> came from.
</p><p>ز زابل به کابل رسید آن زمان<br />
From <a href="/wiki/Zabul" class="mw-redirect" title="Zabul">Zabul</a> he arrived to <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a><br />
گرازان و خندان و دل شادمان<br />
Strutting, happy, and mirthful<br />
<i>---<a href="/wiki/Ferdowsi" title="Ferdowsi">Ferdowsi</a> in <a href="/wiki/Shahnama" class="mw-redirect" title="Shahnama">Shahnama</a></i>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pakistan">Pakistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Pakistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There is considerable influence of Iranian-speaking peoples in Pakistan. The region of Baluchistan is split between Pakistan and Iran and Baluchi, the majority languages of the Baluchistan province of Pakistan are also spoken in Southeastern Iran. In fact, the Chagai Hills and the western part of Makran district were part of Iran till the <a href="/wiki/Durand_Line" title="Durand Line">Durand Line</a> was drawn in the late 1800s.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pashto_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Pashto language">Pashto</a> which is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa" title="Khyber Pakhtunkhwa">Khyber Pakhtunkhwa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Federally_Administered_Tribal_Areas" title="Federally Administered Tribal Areas">FATA</a> of Pakistan and Afghanistan is an Iranian language.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_and_modern_maps_of_Iran">Historical and modern maps of Iran</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Historical and modern maps of Iran">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treaties">Treaties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Iran&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Treaties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Amasya" title="Peace of Amasya">1555 Treaty of Amasya</a>: The first treaty between Safavid Persia and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, splitting the Caucasus and Mesopotamia in a Turkish and Persian sphere.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Zuhab" title="Treaty of Zuhab">1639 Treaty of Zuhab</a>: Iran loses Iraq to the Ottoman Empire.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Gulistan" title="Treaty of Gulistan">1813 Gulestan Treaty</a>: Iran loses a large amount of its land in the Caucasus, including eastern half of Georgia, southern <a href="/wiki/Dagestan" title="Dagestan">Dagestan</a>, large parts of the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Armenian Republic">Armenian Republic</a>, and most of what is today the <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Azerbaijan Republic">Azerbaijan Republic</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Turkmenchay" title="Treaty of Turkmenchay">1828 Turkmenchay Treaty</a>: Signed by <a href="/wiki/Fath_Ali_Shah" class="mw-redirect" title="Fath Ali Shah">Fath Ali Shah</a>. Russia gains sovereignty over the entire Caucasus, including Iran's <a href="/wiki/Nakhchivan_Autonomous_Republic" title="Nakhchivan Autonomous Republic">Nakhichivan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nagorno-Karabakh" title="Nagorno-Karabakh">Nagorno-Karabakh</a>, the entirety of <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, and the remainder of the modern-day territory of the Republic of Azerbaijan</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/1857_Paris_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="1857 Paris Treaty">1857 Paris Treaty</a>: Signed by <a href="/wiki/Nasereddin_Shah" class="mw-redirect" title="Nasereddin Shah">Nasereddin Shah</a>. Iran renounces all claims to <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a> and parts of Afghanistan in exchange for the evacuation of Iran's southern ports by Great Britain.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Akhal" title="Treaty of Akhal">1881 Akhal Treaty</a>: Signed by <a href="/wiki/Nasereddin_Shah" class="mw-redirect" title="Nasereddin Shah">Nasereddin Shah</a>. Iran loses <a href="/wiki/Merv" title="Merv">Merv</a> and parts of <a href="/wiki/Khwarazmia" class="mw-redirect" title="Khwarazmia">Khwarazmia</a> in exchange for security guarantees from Russia.</li>
<li>1893: Iran transfers to <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Russia">Russia</a> additional regions near the <a href="/wiki/Atrek_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Atrek River">Atrek River</a> that were Iranian under the Akhal Treaty. This treaty was signed by General Boutsoff and <i>Mirza Ali Asghar Amin al-Sultan</i> on May 27, 1893.</li>
<li>1907: Persia was to be carved up into three regions, according to the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Russian_Convention_of_1907" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Russian Convention of 1907">Anglo-Russian Convention of 1907</a>.</li>
<li>1970: Iran abandons sovereignty rights over <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a> to Great Britain in exchange for <a href="/wiki/Greater_and_Lesser_Tunbs" title="Greater and Lesser Tunbs">Greater and Lesser Tunbs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abu_Musa" title="Abu Musa">Abu Musa</a> islands in the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>.</li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Richard Nelson Frye, The Harvard Theological Review, Vol. 55, No. 4 (Oct., 1962), pp. 261-268 <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="https://www.jstor.org/pss/1508723">https://www.jstor.org/pss/1508723</a> I use the term Iran in an historical context[...]Persia would be used for the modern state, more or less equivalent to "western Iran". I use the term "Greater Iran" to mean what I suspect most Classicists and ancient historians really mean by their use of Persia - that which was within the political boundaries of States ruled by Iranians.</span>

However, it is important to note that during the Achaemeid era, the official language of the empire was <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a>, which was the mother tongue of the ancient [Iraqis], since it was the language of literature, religion, and science at that time. <li id="cite_note-pol-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-pol_62-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-pol_62-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Fuccaro, Nelida (2009-09-03). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/?id=wKU6jvKGicUC&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;dq#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false"><i>Histories of City and State in the Persian Gulf: Manama Since 1800</i></a>. p.&#160;114. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-521-51435-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-521-51435-4"><bdi>978-0-521-51435-4</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Histories+of+City+and+State+in+the+Persian+Gulf%3A+Manama+Since+1800&amp;rft.pages=114&amp;rft.date=2009-09-03&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-521-51435-4&amp;rft.aulast=Fuccaro&amp;rft.aufirst=Nelida&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2F%3Fid%3DwKU6jvKGicUC%26printsec%3Dfrontcover%26dq%23v%3Donepage%26q%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreater+Iran" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-EY-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-EY_63-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-EY_63-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-EY_63-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Ehsan_Yarshater" title="Ehsan Yarshater">Yarshater, Ehsan</a> (1993). <i>The Cambridge History of Iran, Volume 3</i>. <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_Press" title="Cambridge University Press">Cambridge University Press</a>. p.&#160;482. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-521-20092-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-521-20092-9"><bdi>978-0-521-20092-9</bdi></a>. <q>Of the four residences of the Achaemenids named by <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>—<a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">Ecbatana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pasargadae" title="Pasargadae">Pasargadae</a> or <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>—the last [situated in Iraq] was maintained as their most important capital, the fixed winter quarters, the central office of bureaucracy, exchanged only in the heat of summer for some cool spot in the highlands. Under the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucids</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a> the site of the Mesopotamian capital moved a little to the north on the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>—to <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a>. It is indeed symbolic that these new foundations were built from the bricks of ancient <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>, just as later <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, a little further upstream, was built out of the ruins of the <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanian</a> double city of <a href="/wiki/Al-Mada%27in" title="Al-Mada&#39;in">Seleucia-Ctesiphon</a>.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Cambridge+History+of+Iran%2C+Volume+3&amp;rft.pages=482&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1993&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-521-20092-9&amp;rft.aulast=Yarshater&amp;rft.aufirst=Ehsan&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreater+Iran" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Richard_N._Frye" title="Richard N. Frye">Frye, Richard N.</a> (1975). <i>The Golden Age of Persia: The Arabs in the East</i>. Weidenfeld and Nicolson. p.&#160;184. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-7538-0944-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-7538-0944-0"><bdi>978-0-7538-0944-0</bdi></a>. <q>[..] throughout Iran's history the western part of the land has been frequently more closely connected with the lowlands of Mesopotamia than with the rest of the plateau to the east of the central deserts.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Golden+Age+of+Persia%3A+The+Arabs+in+the+East&amp;rft.pages=184&amp;rft.pub=Weidenfeld+and+Nicolson&amp;rft.date=1975&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-7538-0944-0&amp;rft.aulast=Frye&amp;rft.aufirst=Richard+N.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGreater+Iran" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-NY-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-NY_65-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NY_65-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Yavari, Neguin (1997). <i>Iranian Perspectives on the Iran-Iraq War; Part II. <li id="cite_note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a href="/wiki/Michael_G._Morony" title="Michael G. Morony">Morony, Michael G</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.iranicaonline.org/articles/iraq-i-late-sasanid-early-islamic">"IRAQ AND ITS RELATIONS WITH IRAN"</a>. <i>IRAQ i.
